BOARD OF DIRECTORS REPORT
Committee Meeting Date:
Salaried Trustee Appointment

November 11, 2025
Board Meeting Date:
December 2, 2025

RECOMMENDED ACTION
For the Board of Directors to approve Resolution No. ______, Series of 2025, reappointing Bill Sirois,
Senior Manager, Transit Oriented Communities, and appointing Brenden Morgan, Director, Treasury,
with terms expiring December 31, 2027, to the Salaried Employee Defined Benefit Pension Plan (DB
plan) and Salaried Employee Defined Contribution Retirement Plan (DC plan) Trusts.
STAFF REPRESENTATIVE
Kelly Mackey, Chief Financial Officer
PRESENTATION LENGTH
5 minutes
BACKGROUND
On January 8, 2008, the Board of Directors adopted Resolution No. 001, Series of 2008 (Resolution No.
1), which established a committee composed of the Board Chair, the Chair of the Salaried Employee
Pension Trust Fund and the Finance Chair (i.e., the Chair of the Finance Administration and Audit
Committee as it existed at that time) to screen applications from prospective pension and DC plan
Trustees from among “the middle management salaried employee class and the senior management
salaried employee class and…[to] bring recommendations back to the Board.”
As a revision to the process established in Resolution No. 1, the Board adopted Resolution No. 27,
Series of 2010, which stated that, “for reappointment of Trustees who have served satisfactorily and in
whose education the Trusts have made substantial investments, it is not necessary to seek applications
for renewal, and instead the application process established by the Board in 2008 would be used on
those occasions when the Board finds it necessary or appropriate to appoint new salaried Trustees to
the Trusts.”
On May 31, 2016, in Resolution No. 008, Series of 2016 (Resolution No. 8), the RTD Board of Directors
amended the Defined Benefit Pension Trust and the Defined Contribution Retirement Trust (Trusts,
collectively) to remove the “senior” and “middle” management distinction established in Resolution
No. 1. Instead, Resolution No. 8 authorized the Board to appoint two employee Trustees who
participate in the pension plan and two who participate in the DC plan, as well as two Directors
appointed to the Trusts and the Chief Financial Officer who is a permanent member of the Trusts, to
serve as the same Trustees on both trusts.
DISCUSSION

Packet Pg. 3

1.D.1

In adopting this Recommended Action, the agency demonstrates its commitment to meet the Strategic
Priorities of Employee Ownership and Financial Success.
RTD provides a defined benefit pension plan to non-represented (salaried) employees hired before
January 1, 2008, and a defined contribution retirement plan to salaried employees hired thereafter.
Each plan is overseen by a Board of Trustees comprised of four RTD staff, two RTD Board members
and the RTD Chief Financial Officer serving as a permanent Trustee. RTD staff Trustees are
recommended to the RTD Board by the General Manager and CEO for appointment and the RTD Board
Chair, with full RTD Board approval, appoints two RTD Board members as Trustees.
This action item seeks the following for Board approval:
• Extend through December 31, 2027, the term of current Trustee Bill Sirois, Senior Manager,
Transit Oriented Communities, whose term expires on December 31, 2025
• Replace Charlene Polege, Chief People Officer, who is departing RTD in December 2025, with
Brenden Morgan, Director, Treasury, with a term expiring December 31, 2027
Trustees serve staggered terms of two years. The above actions will retain a body of seven Trustees.
The term extension and appointment of these Trustees will focus Trust governance with employees
who participate in and represent staff colleagues participating in these retirement plans, have expertise
in the area of finance, as well as maintain Board participation in the salaried employee retirement
Trusts in its capacity as a policy-making body.
Further, the Trust plan documents will continue to require Board approval for changes in plan design
and annual budget appropriation in addition to any material changes to the plans coming to the Board
through the Audit Committee.
FINANCIAL IMPACT
There is no financial impact associated with the adoption of this Recommended Action.

BOARD OF DIRECTORS REPORT
Committee Meeting Date:
Ticket Vending Machines Modernization Title VI Fare
Equity Analysis

November 11, 2025
Board Meeting Date:
December 2, 2025

RECOMMENDED ACTION
For the Board of Directors (Board) to adopt the Title VI Fare Equity Analysis report for the proposed
addition, reduction and replacement strategy for Ticket Vending Machines (TVMs), to comply with
federal laws, regulations and guidelines related to Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964.
STAFF REPRESENTATIVES
Carl Green Jr., Director, Civil Rights Division
Martin Romero, Transit Equity Manager, Civil Rights Division
Justin Harding, Civil Rights and Equity Analyst
PRESENTATION LENGTH
15 minutes
BACKGROUND

Fare Modernization
RTD continues to advance its fare modernization initiative to improve customer experience, enhance
system reliability and reduce fare collection costs. This includes implementation of tap-to-pay and a
cash digitization retail network, for which the Board approved the Title VI Fare Equity Analysis on
September 30, 2025.
To support modernization goals and optimize TVM utilization, RTD proposes the following changes:
•
•
•
•
•

Reducing TVMs across the system from 185 to 109, including removing all 15 TVMs along the
Flatiron Flyer corridor
Adding two TVMs per platform bidirectionally along the East Colfax Bus Rapid Transit (BRT)
corridor for a total of 54
Adding six TVMs in the Denver Union Station Bus Concourse
Adding one TVM at Civic Center Station
Replacing remaining TVMs and adding new TVMs with smaller, cash-capable models

Customers will continue to have access to onboard cash and MyRide payment options.

Title VI Fare Equity Analysis Policies
The Federal Transit Administration (FTA) Circular 4702.1B requires federally funded transit providers to
evaluate proposed fare changes prior to implementation to determine whether they would result in

Packet Pg. 8

1.D.2

disparate impacts (DI) on minority (hereafter referred to as Black, Indigenous and People of Color, or
BIPOC) populations or disproportionate burdens (DB) on low-income populations. On May 28, 2025, the
Board adopted RTD’s 2025-2028 Title VI Program, which clarified the agency’s definition of a fare
change to include modifications to Ticket Vending Machine access, such as changes in the number of
machines, fees or payment options.
Pursuant to FTA Circular 4702.1B Chapter IV.7, RTD must establish a Disparate Impact Policy and a
Disproportionate Burden Policy. Collectively, these policies provide foundational requirements for
evaluating fare change proposals for equity and compliance with Title VI. The policies and their
applicable thresholds are defined as follows:
Disparate Impact Policy: A fare change should not adversely affect BIPOC populations 5% more
than the overall population ridership; this level of impact is considered a disparate
impact. “BIPOC” is defined as all persons who identify as being part of racial/ethnic groups
besides white, non-Hispanic.
Disproportionate Burden Policy: A fare change should not adversely affect low-income
populations 5% more than the overall population ridership; this level of impact is considered a
disproportionate burden. A low-income population is a group of households who are at or below
200% of the Department of Health and Human Services Poverty Guidelines.
The purpose of this analysis is to assess whether the proposed fare change would result in DI on BIPOC
populations or DB on low-income populations. When a proposed fare change results in a DI, RTD must
consider alternatives to avoid, minimize or mitigate the impact; demonstrate a substantial legitimate
justification for the change and ensure that no less-discriminatory alternatives exist that would achieve
the same program goals. In cases where a fare change results in a DB on low-income populations, RTD
will consider practicable modifications to mitigate the impact as necessary.
DISCUSSION
Because the proposed TVM changes meet RTD’s Title VI Program definition of a fare change, a Fare
Equity Analysis was required. The complete analysis is provided in Attachment A.
RTD evaluated the proposed changes across four key TVM optimization strategies: 1) TVM reduction, 2)
replacement of remaining TVMs with small-footprint models that match current functionality, 3)
Flatiron Flyer removal and 4) East Colfax BRT placement. Each strategy was assessed for potential DI
on BIPOC populations and DB on low-income populations.
The proposed reduction of TVMs involves removing 61 machines across the system (not including
Flatiron Flyer) while retaining at least one per station. TVM usage is low systemwide, representing 16%
of total fare payments in 2024, with comparable usage among BIPOC and low-income customers. Staff
identified 13 stations that warrant retaining two machines due to higher BIPOC and low-income
demographics, higher cash sales, accessibility needs and/or customer volume.

Packet Pg. 9

1.D.2

The replacement of existing TVMs with smaller models requires careful consideration of fare payment
equity. To ensure equitable access for customers who rely on cash, staff recommend that each smallfootprint TVM be replaced with a cash-capable model. All new or replacement machines must also
meet RTD’s accessibility and language access standards, including support for English and Spanish,
Braille, and audible prompts.

Flatiron Flyer Corridor
For the Flatiron Flyer corridor, RTD proposes removing all 15 TVMs. Utilization is minimal, with fewer
than 2% of customers indicating that they used these machines as their payment method. Along the
corridor stations with TVMs, one of the eight stations (US 36·Sheridan) serves higher-than-average
BIPOC populations, and three (Downtown Boulder, Boulder Junction and US 36·Table Mesa) serve
higher-than-average low-income populations.

East Colfax BRT
The East Colfax BRT corridor has distinct equity considerations. The corridor serves a diverse customer
base, with 63.6% of onboard survey respondents identifying as BIPOC and 60.6% as low-income. Data
from the 2024 Onboard Transit Customer Survey shows high reliance on cash payment among
customers traveling this corridor. Systemwide, 26.7% of respondents reported paying fares with cash
onboard, compared with 31.4% among BIPOC customers and 33.2% among low-income customers.
Among the 1,240 surveyed customers on Routes 15 and 15L, cash use was higher, with 41.3% of all
respondents, 45.1% of BIPOC and 47.3% of low-income customers using cash payment.
Methodology:
Staff evaluated potential equity impacts of RTD’s TVM modernization plan using multiple data sources
consistent with FTA Title VI Circular 4702.1B Chapter IV-19 guidance. Data sources included the 2024
Onboard Transit Customer Survey, 2019-2023 American Community Survey (ACS) five-year estimates,
2024 station-level boardings and ramp deployments, TVM cash sales data and RTD’s cash digitization
retail network. These sources informed both system-level and station-level analyses and supported the
creation of a composite index to guide decisions on TVM placement and retention.

System-Level
At the system level, the onboard survey provided a representative view of fare payment methods and
customer demographics across RTD’s service area. Survey responses were analyzed by fare payment
method and demographic characteristics, including BIPOC and low-income status, to support systemlevel DI/DB calculations.

Flatiron Flyer Corridor
For the Flatiron Flyer corridor, a separate analysis was conducted. TVM use was limited among
surveyed customers, so a proportional demographic analysis using ACS data was applied. This review
compared the share of BIPOC and low-income populations within a half mile of Flatiron Flyer stations
against the broader service area. The half-mile buffer aligns with standard FTA and Metropolitan
Planning Organization (MPO) practices for capturing populations likely to access stations.

Packet Pg. 10

1.D.2

Station-Level Analysis
At the station level, a weighted composite index score was developed to evaluate potential equity
impacts. Six key metrics were included: BIPOC population, low-income population, customer
boardings, ramp deployments, cash sales and proximity to retailers. Each metric was standardized
using quartile rankings and weighted by its relevance to Title VI considerations: BIPOC (30%), lowincome (20%), boardings (15%), ramp deployments (15%), cash sales (15%) and retailer proximity (5%).
Scores were normalized to a five-point scale to allow comparability across stations.

Accessibility Review
Ramp deployment data were included as a proxy for accessibility needs. Additionally, the Americans
with Disabilities Act (ADA) Office conducted a desktop review of station renderings to identify potential
concerns related to TVM reductions or relocations, including access to platforms, station entries and
exits, parking and bus loading areas.
Summary of Findings:
Systemwide TVM utilization remains low. Of 18,285 onboard survey respondents, 12,614 answered the
fare payment question. Overall, 9.3% reported using a TVM, with 8.5% of BIPOC customers and 8.6% of
low-income customers using TVMs. Among rail users (4,183 respondents), TVM use is higher: 20.2%
overall, 20.7% of BIPOC customers and 20.0% of low-income customers. These figures do not meet
RTD’s 5% threshold for disparate impact (DI) or disproportionate burden (DB); thus, there is no DI or DB
finding.
At the station level, a composite index score was calculated for each station using demographic,
ridership, accessibility, cash sales and retailer proximity data. Most stations scored between 0 and 3,
indicating low to moderate equity concern. Stations (with numerous existing TVMs) scoring 4 or higher
were prioritized for retaining two TVMs: Lakewood/Wadsworth, Decatur/Federal, Aurora Metro Center,
40th/Colorado and 61st/Peña. The ADA Office’s accessibility review further recommended retaining two
TVMs at Colfax, Peoria, Auraria West, Clear Creek/Federal, I-25/Broadway, Southmoor, Wheat
Ridge/Ward Road and Louisiana/Pearl stations. To maintain equitable access, RTD will retain at least
one TVM per station, retain two at high-priority stations (where two or more currently exist), and
ensure all new or replacement units meet accessibility and language access standards. Maintaining
cash-capable TVMs will address barriers for unbanked or underbanked customers.

Flatiron Flyer Corridor
The 15 TVMs along the Flatiron Flyer corridor are proposed for full removal. Onboard survey results
show very low TVM usage overall (1.9%), with 3.5% of BIPOC customers and 1.8% of low-income
customers using the machines. Only one of the eight stations (US36/Sheridan) serve a higher-thanaverage BIPOC population, and three stations (Downtown Boulder, Boulder Junction, and US36/Table
Mesa) serve a higher-than-average low-income population. Because survey responses were limited, a
proportional analysis using 2019-2023 ACS data was conducted. This analysis showed minimal
differences between BIPOC and non-BIPOC populations (0.39% vs. 0.69%) and a slightly higher impact
on low-income populations versus non-low-income populations (0.90% vs. 0.48%). Cash and MyRide

Packet Pg. 11

1.D.2

payment options are available onboard, mitigating fare access barriers. The composite index score for
these stations averaged 1.5, with none exceeding 2.3, confirming limited equity concern.

East Colfax BRT
For the East Colfax BRT corridor, RTD will install two TVMs per platform. This meets FTA Small Starts
Grant requirements and ensures off-board fare purchase options on one of RTD’s most diverse and
transit-dependent corridors.
Conclusion and Recommendation:
The Title VI Fare Equity Analysis finds that RTD’s proposed TVM modernization strategy does not result
in DI on BIPOC populations or DB on low-income populations.

Systemwide
TVM utilization remains low systemwide. Onboard survey data indicate that usage differences among
all respondents, BIPOC customers and low-income customers do not meet RTD’s 5% thresholds for a
disparate impact or disproportionate burden finding. Localized analysis using the composite index
identified 13 high-priority stations where two TVMs should be retained based on ridership, cash usage
and accessibility needs. Cash-capable, accessible and multilingual machines at these locations ensure
equitable access.

Flatiron Flyer Corridor
TVM usage along the Flatiron Flyer corridor is minimal. Onboard survey data and supplemental ACSbased proportional analysis indicate that the proposed removal of all 15 TVMs will have negligible
impacts on BIPOC and low-income populations. Customers retain onboard cash and MyRide payment
options, mitigating any potential fare access barriers.

East Colfax BRT
For the East Colfax BRT corridor, two TVMs will be installed per platform. Machines will be cashcapable and offer multilingual access, consistent with FTA Small Starts Grant requirements and RTD’s
Title VI commitments. This approach ensures equitable fare access along one of RTD’s most diverse
and transit-dependent corridors.

Denver Union Station and Civic Center Station
RTD intends to develop a closed-system design for the Denver Union Station Bus Concourse. Six TVMs
will be needed to support this design. In addition, RTD staff requested installing one TVM at Civic
Center Station. These objectives will be incorporated into the agency’s overall TVM strategy following
the review and approval of this analysis.
The proposed actions support RTD’s strategic goals of improving customer experience, maintaining
system reliability and efficiently managing fare collection resources. RTD concludes that the proposed
fare change does not result in an inequitable distribution of benefits or burdens. If the Board approves
the Title VI Analysis, the proposed fare change will proceed as proposed.

Packet Pg. 12

1.D.2

FINANCIAL IMPACT
The adoption of the Title VI Fare Equity Analysis will not result in any direct or foreseeable financial
impacts.

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

Executive Summary
RTD conducted a Title VI Fare Equity Analysis to evaluate whether proposed changes to the agency’s
ticket vending machines (TVMs) would result in disparate impacts (DI) on Black, Indigenous and other
People of Color (BIPOC) populations or disproportionate burdens (DB) on low-income populations. The
analysis reviewed proposed modifications to TVMs across the system, including their reduction and
replacement at the system level, full removal along the Flatiron Flyer corridor and addition along the
East Colfax Bus Rapid Transit (BRT) corridor.

Summary of Findings
System-Level Findings
Analysis of 2024 Onboard Transit Customer Survey data found that TVM usage among all customers is
low (9.3%), with comparable rates among BIPOC (8.5%) and low-income (8.6%) customers. These
results indicate no DI or DB associated with the proposed system-wide reduction of TVMs. However,
due to nonresponse from nearly one-third of survey participants, staff supplemented survey data with
a station-level analysis to ensure a more complete evaluation.
Station-Level Findings
A composite index score—incorporating demographic, ridership, accessibility and retail access data—
was developed to assess station-level equity implications. Most stations scored low, suggesting limited
concern for equity or accessibility impacts. Thirteen stations with two or more existing TVMs received
higher composite scores (four or greater) or accessibility considerations and were identified for
retaining more than one TVM to maintain equitable access. These stations include
Lakewood●Wadsworth, Decatur●Federal, Aurora Metro Center, 40th●Colorado, 61st●Peña, Colfax,
Peoria, Auraria West, Clear Creek●Federal, I-25●Broadway, Southmoor, Wheat Ridge●Ward Road and
Louisiana●Pearl.
Flatiron Flyer Findings
According to RTD’s 2024 onboard survey results, TVM use along the Flatiron Flyer corridor is extremely
low (1.9% overall, 3.5% among BIPOC, 1.8% among low-income customers). Due to limited survey
responses, proportional analysis using 2019-2023 American Community Survey (ACS) data was
conducted as a supplement. Results showed only marginally more low-income populations may be
impacted than non-low-income populations, which staff deemed negligible (0.48% versus 0.90%,
respectively). Overall, removal of the 15 TVMs along the Flatiron Flyer corridor presents minimal equity
concern.
East Colfax BRT Findings
Findings from the 2024 Onboard Transit Customer Survey show that cash payment remains an essential
option for many RTD customers, particularly on the Colfax corridor. Systemwide, roughly one in four
customers pays fares with cash, rising to nearly half of customers on Routes 15 and 15L, one of RTD’s
most diverse and transit-dependent corridors. These results highlight the need to maintain both
onboard and off-board cash payment options to ensure equitable fare access as RTD transitions to
modernized payment systems.
2

Actions
Staff intend to pursue the following:
•

•

•

•

Systemwide TVM reduction. Proceed with reduction of TVMs, ensuring at least one machine
remains at each station (excluding Flatiron Flyer stations). Retain two machines at 13 priority
stations identified through the composite index and accessibility review and keep the lower-level
TVM at Downtown Littleton Station for accessibility purposes.
Replacement with small footprint TVMs. Support replacement if new machines are cashcapable. Machines should meet accessibility standards and include language access in English,
Spanish and all safe harbor languages.
Flatiron Flyer TVM removal. Removal is supported due to redundancy with onboard payment
options and low overall usage. However, the agency may wish to retain high-revenue machines
at Downtown Boulder and US 36●Table Mesa stations or other Flatiron Flyer TVMs for reasons
beyond equity.
East Colfax BRT implementation. Support installation of a cash-capable TVM at each stop
bidirectionally to ensure equitable access to fare media for this diverse ridership base as well as
to meet the requirement of the Small Starts Grant funding the project.

Finally, RTD intends to develop a closed-system design for the Denver Union Station Bus Concourse.
Six TVMs will be needed to support this design. In addition, staff requested adding one TVM at Civic
Center Station. These directives will be incorporated into the agency’s overall TVM modernization
strategy following the review and adoption of this analysis.

Conclusion
Staff find that the proposed TVM strategy does not result in any DI on BIPOC populations or
disproportionate burden on low-income populations. With the described mitigations in place—
particularly ensuring accessibility and station-level retention where warranted—the proposed TVM plan
advances RTD’s fare modernization goals while maintaining equitable access across the system.

Background
RTD is advancing its fare collection strategy to improve customer experience and reduce the cost of
collecting fares. This strategy balances near-term updates with future-oriented planning to meet the
needs of diverse customers while aligning infrastructure and technology decisions with this agency
priority.
A key component of this strategy is the replacement and optimization of TVMs across the RTD system.
In the initial proposal, RTD had outlined plans to replace 185 outdated and non-compliant TVMs and
procure an additional 54 TVMs for the East Colfax BRT line. Early estimates also indicated that the cost
of collecting fares through TVMs is significantly higher than other fare collection methods, costing more
than $0.21 for every dollar received compared with $0.04-$0.09 for alternatives such as mobile ticketing
and onboard fareboxes.1
1

RTD also offers several fare payment options that can be pre-purchased with cash or credit/debit cards, including paper
ticket books and passes, mobile tickets and MyRide stored value.

3

rtd-denver.com

Packet Pg. 17

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

As a result, RTD is now proposing four actions to ensure that the procurement of TVMs is aligned with
RTD’s fare collection strategy:
1. Reduce the number of TVMs at existing stations
2. Replace remaining TVMs with smaller footprint TVMs
3. Remove TVMs from Flatiron Flyer stations
4. Optimize the number of TVMs for the East Colfax BRT
Given that the proposed reduction of TVMs constitutes a fare change as defined in RTD’s 2025-2028
Title VI Program, a Title VI Fare Equity Analysis must be completed.

Title VI Fare Equity Analysis Requirements
Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964
Title VI prohibits discrimination on the basis of race, color or national origin in federally funded
programs. Specifically, Title VI states, “No person in the United States shall, on the ground of race,
color or national origin, be excluded from participation in, be denied the benefits of, or be subjected to
discrimination under any program or activity receiving Federal financial assistance.” The Federal Transit
Administration (FTA) implements this statute through Circular 4702.1B, Title VI Requirements and
Guidelines for Federal Transit Administration Recipients, which provides guidance on how transit
providers that receive federal funding must comply with Title VI regulations.
Title VI Requirements to Conduct a Fare Equity Analysis
FTA Circular 4702.1B Chapter IV.4 requires transit agencies to conduct a Title VI fare equity analysis
whenever fare or major service changes are proposed. The purpose of the analysis is to ensure that
proposed changes do not result in discriminatory impacts, including a DI on BIPOC populations and/or
a DB on low-income populations.2 Considerations in the analysis include whether fare structures place
a higher burden on protected groups than on the overall ridership, whether customers have equitable
access to specialized fare media and whether communication methods adequately reach linguistically
diverse populations.
RTD’s Fare Equity Analysis Policy
In compliance with FTA Circular 4702.1B Chapter IV.7, RTD maintains a fare Disparate Impact Policy
and a Disproportionate Burden Policy within its 2025-2028 Title VI Program. The most recent update
refined the definition of a fare change and retained existing thresholds used to determine whether
proposed changes result in inequitable impacts on BIPOC or low-income populations compared with
overall ridership.
RTD defines a fare change to include:
•
•

Increases or decreases in the price of fare media
Introduction or discontinuation of fare media (e.g., cash, paper pass, or electronic)

2

RTD considers households earning at or below 200% of the Department of Health and Human Services Poverty Guidelines
to be low income.

4

rtd-denver.com

Packet Pg. 18

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

•
•
•

Changes in fare types or products (e.g., Day Pass, Monthly Pass)
Adjustments to fare levels (e.g., Standard, Airport) or customer categories (e.g., full-fare,
seniors, individuals with disabilities, Medicare recipients, LiVE program participants and youth
ages 19 and under)
Modifications to TVM access, such as changes in quantity, fees or payment options

Access-a-Ride services are excluded from this definition and are not subject to Title VI fare equity
requirements.
Under RTD’s DI and DB policies, a DI is found when a proposed fare change affects BIPOC populations
5% more than the overall ridership, and a DB is found when a change affects low-income populations
5% more than overall ridership. If an analysis identifies a DI or DB, RTD evaluates whether
modifications can avoid, minimize or mitigate the impact. A fare change with a DI may proceed only if
there is a substantial legitimate justification and no less discriminatory alternative can achieve the
agency’s goals. When a DB is identified, RTD will consider adjusting the proposal to mitigate the impact
where practicable.
The following section outlines the initial proposed modifications to the agency’s TVMs.

Initial Proposed Changes
Removal of 89 TVMs at Existing Stations
The proposal recommends reducing the total number of TVMs across the system, ensuring that at least
one TVM remains at each station with existing machines, with the exception of Flatiron Flyer stations
(see below). In total, 893 machines are proposed for removal, including 25 that are credit/debit cardonly.
Replacement of 96 Machines with Small Footprint TVMs
If the agency proceeds with reducing the overall number of TVMs across the system, while maintaining
at least one TVM at each station in each direction of travel where currently applicable, the next
recommendation is to replace the remaining 96 TVMs with models that have a smaller footprint.
Removal of all TVMs at Flatiron Flyer Stations
The final proposed change is to remove all TVMs located at Flatiron Flyer stations. This proposal is
supported by two considerations. First, customers can already purchase fares with cash directly
onboard Flatiron Flyer vehicles or with credit/debit cards using mobile tickets and MyRide stored value
prior to boarding, making the presence of TVMs redundant. Second, the planned launch of tap-to-pay
open payment in the fourth quarter of 2025 will allow customers to purchase fares on board using
contactless credit or debit cards or mobile wallets. This change is expected to render TVMs largely
obsolete, particularly given that in 2024 an average of 71%of fares purchased at TVMs along the
Flatiron Flyer route were paid with credit or debit cards.

3

Including the newly reopened Boulder Junction at Depot Square Station and Flatiron Flyer stations.

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

Optimize the Number of TVMs for East Colfax BRT
To maintain compliance with the Federal Transit Administration’s Small Starts Grant funding for the
East Colfax BRT project, RTD is required to install one TVM at each Colfax BRT stop bidirectionally.
This requirement will support access to off-board fare purchases for all customers.

Methodology
To understand which customers may be impacted by modifications to TVMs throughout the service
area, staff considered several data sources that capture the demographics and travel behavior of
customers:
•
•
•
•
•
•

RTD’s Onboard Transit Customer Survey (2024)
U.S. Census Bureau’s 2019-2023 American Community Survey (ACS) five-year estimates
Average station/stop ramp deployments (January 2024 to January 2025)
Customer boardings (January 2024 to January 2025)
TVM sales data (2024)
RTD retail network (2024)

Onboard data informed the necessary DI/DB calculations for proposed changes at the system level
and, separately, along the Flatiron Flyer route.4 The remaining data informed the creation of a
composite index score, described later in this methodology, to evaluate the impacts of the proposed
changes at the station level. Finally, the Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA) Office conducted a
desktop accessibility review of station renderings to flag concerns that may arise from removing specific
TVMs.

System-Level, Flatiron Flyer, and East Colfax BRT Analyses
Onboard Transit Customer Survey
From August 19 to December 10, 2024, RTD conducted a systemwide onboard customer survey
providing a snapshot of weekday trip characteristics across 99 bus routes and 10 rail lines with 18,285
customer responses (excluding visitors). The survey resulted in 66% sample of trips for bus and 33%
sample for rail. Data was weighted to the estimated weekday ridership of 170,000 originating rides.
This data informed the system-level analysis to determine if the proposed systemwide reduction of
TVMs would result in a DI or DB, as required by the FTA.
The DI and DB calculations for the proposed full-scale removal of TVMs along the Flatiron Flyer were
conducted separately. However, the 2024 onboard survey data showed that only 1.9% of Flatiron Flyer
customers used TVMs, with very few BIPOC and low-income respondents answering this question in
the survey. Because the sample size was too small to produce statistically valid results, the onboard
4

The Transit Equity Office relied on onboard survey data for these disparate impact and disproportionate burden calculations
because FTA Circular 4702.1B instructs transit providers to analyze ridership-survey information on fare media usage to
identify whether BIPOC or low-income customers use the affected payment methods more heavily. Specifically, Circular
Section IV.7 Data Analysis, “For proposed changes … the transit provider shall analyze any available information generated
from ridership surveys indicating whether minority and/or low-income riders are disproportionately more likely to use the
mode of service, payment type, or payment media that would be subject to the fare change.”

6

rtd-denver.com

Packet Pg. 20

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

data could not reliably inform this portion of the analysis. Instead, a proportional analysis using 20192023 American Community Survey data was conducted to assess potential equity impacts by comparing
the share of BIPOC and low-income residents in census block groups within a half mile of Flatiron Flyer
stations to those across the broader service area.
The onboard survey was also used to inform analysis of TVMs proposed for the East Colfax BRT—
specifically, fare payment methods for customers on the 15/15L, which are the routes most similar to
future East Colfax BRT routing.

Station-Level Analysis
Composite Index Score
Staff wanted to evaluate how proposed changes to TVMs may affect Title VI populations at the station
level in addition to system-level DI/DB calculations. To do so, the team calculated a weighted composite
index score for each station. This score integrates six key metrics:
•

•

•

•

•

Demographic data on race/ethnicity and income. A proportional analysis using 2019-2023
ACS five-year estimates was conducted to evaluate whether removing TVMs would
disproportionately affect BIPOC and/or low-income populations. Demographic data was mapped
to census block groups within a half-mile radius of each station.
Customer volume (i.e., average boardings). Customer boarding volumes from 2024
provide essential context for understanding overall station activity. Evaluating boardings helps
determine where TVMs are most needed to support fare purchases and maintain equitable
access to cash payment options. High ridership locations may warrant retaining additional TVMs
to prevent undue barriers for customers.
Average ramp deployments. Because ramp usage reflects how frequently customers require
mobility assistance or devices board at each location, the inclusion of average ramp deployment
data (2024) ensures that accessibility is considered in evaluating TVM removals.
Cash sales. Station-level sales data from 2024 helps identify where customers are most reliant
on TVMs. Because BIPOC and low-income customers are more likely to be unbanked or prefer
cash payments, this data offers a more accurate picture of how TVM removals may affect equity
populations.
Proximity of nearby retailers. Approximately 130 King Soopers and Safeway stores across
the metro area sell RTD fare media, including ticket books and monthly passes. The number of
retailers within a half-mile radius around each station were calculated and, in effect, act as a
mitigating factor in the case of a proposed TVM removal and/or replacement.

Each of the six metrics was converted to a standardized score using quartile ranking, where stations in
the lowest quartile received a score of 0 and those in the highest quartile received a score of 3. Specific
scoring logic varied slightly by metric; for example, stations with fewer nearby retailers or higher ramp
deployments received higher scores to indicate greater potential equity concern.
Once individual metric scores were established, staff applied weights to reflect each factor’s relative
importance to Title VI equity considerations. For station-level analysis, weights were assigned as
follows:
7

rtd-denver.com

Packet Pg. 21

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

•
•
•
•
•
•

BIPOC population – 30%
Low-income population – 20%
Boardings – 15%
Ramp deployments – 15%
Cash sales – 15%
Retailers – 5%

Each station’s overall score was calculated using the equation:
Station Score = (BIPOC population x 0.30) + (Low-income population x 0.20) + (Boardings x 0.15) +
(Ramp deployments x 0.15) + (Cash sales x 0.15) + (Retailers x 0.05)
Scores were then normalized to a five-point scale by multiplying by 5/3. Higher scores indicate stations
with greater equity and/or accessibility considerations.
Accessibility Desktop Review
After staff calculated the DI/DB and composite index scores, the ADA Office examined renderings of
all rail stations and assessed the position of TVMs in relation to rail platforms, accessible parking
facilities, station entry and exit points, and, where applicable, bus loading platforms. This review
identified the TVM locations that offer the most accessible opportunities for customers to purchase fare
and allowed the ADA Office to determine whether a TVM removal would cause an undue burden for
people with disabilities.

Results
System-Level Analysis

The first proposal includes an overall reduction in the number of TVMs across the system, where one
TVM would remain at all stations with existing TVMs (except those at Flatiron Flyer stations; see below).
Specifically, 895 were initially recommended for removal, with 25 of those being credit card-only
machines.
To conduct the required DI/DB analysis per the FTA, staff used ridership data collected via the 2024
Onboard Transit Customer Survey. After processing the survey responses, staff found that
approximately 10,092 of 18,285 completed surveys (55%) identified as BIPOC, and 8,800 of 14,359
completed surveys (63%) identified as low-income, using 200% of the federal poverty level as the
threshold.
Respondents were asked, “What fare payment method did you use?”—a question that served as a data
source for this analysis. However, this measure has limitations. Of the 18,285 non-visiting customers
who completed the survey, only 12,614 (69%) responded to the fare payment question. Among those
who did, 9.3% reported using a TVM. TVM usage rates were similar across all respondent groups—
8.5% among BIPOC respondents and 8.6% among low-income respondents—indicating no meaningful
variation by demographic (and no DI or DB finding). These results also indicate that TVM usage is

generally low. When isolating rail customers, usage is higher: 20.2% used TVMs for fare payment, with
BIPOC customers at 20.7% and low-income customers at 20% (no DI or DB finding).

Because nearly one-third of respondents did not answer the fare payment question, the
representativeness of this data is limited. These results should therefore be interpreted with caution;
the station-level analysis that follows, including an accessibility review, helps ensure a more complete
equity assessment.

Station-Level Analysis
To further inform the impact of TVM removal, staff calculated a weighted composite index score for
each station as described in the methodology. Stations receiving lower scores posed the least equity
concern for TVM removal, and those receiving higher scores pose the most equity concern for TVM
removal. Staff designated stations with a score of four or higher as those that may warrant keeping a
second TVM (where multiple TVMs already exist). Additionally, the ADA Office examined renderings
of each station to determine whether a specific TVM’s removal would inhibit accessibility for people
with disabilities.

Flatiron Flyer Analysis
The final proposal is to remove all 15 TVMs at Flatiron Flyer stations. Two reasons are given: first,
that customers can purchase fares with cash and MyRide (mobile tickets and stored value) on board
Flatiron Flyers already, making TVMs redundant; and second, that RTD will launch tap-to-pay open
payment in the fourth quarter of 2025, leaving TVMs largely obsolete for bus customers since they
will be able to purchase tickets on board with credit/debit cards (especially given that in 2024, an
average of 71% of fares purchased across TVMs along the Flatiron Flyer route were paid for by
credit/debit card).
The proposed removal suggests minimal impact on BIPOC and low-income populations, as only one
of the eight (US 36●Sheridan) Flatiron Flyer stops is considered a BIPOC stop, and three of the eight
(Downtown Boulder, US 36●Table Mesa) are considered low-income stops.8

Further, onboard survey data show TVM use along the Flatiron Flyer is low overall (1.9% of customers
selected it as their fare payment method). Among BIPOC customers, use was 3.5%, and among lowincome customers, 1.8%. Both of these results are within the 5% DI and DB thresholds. However,
these figures are too small to be statistically reliable given that only 57 BIPOC and 55 low-income
respondents answered this portion of the survey. A supplemental proportional analysis using ACS
population-based data found that removing TVMs would have less impact on BIPOC populations
compared with non-BIPOC populations (0.39% vs. 0.69%). On the other hand, there is a slightly
higher impact on low-income populations compared with non-low-income populations (0.90% vs.
0.48%). The difference is extremely small, and staff consider equity impacts to be negligible.
The composite index score reinforces that there is limited concern; the average score received by the
Flatiron Flyer stops was 1.5, and none exceeded 2.3 on the five-point scale.

East Colfax BRT Analysis
Data from the 2024 Onboard Transit Customer Survey indicated that, systemwide, 26.7% of
respondents reported paying fares with cash onboard, compared with 31.4% among BIPOC customers
and 33.2% among low-income customers. Analysis of the 1,240 responses from customers on Routes
15 and 15L10 showed even higher reliance on cash: 41.3% of all respondents, 45.1% of BIPOC and
47.3% of low-income customers reported using cash payment. Additionally, Routes 15 and 15L operate
along one of RTD’s most diverse ridership corridors, where 63.6% of 15/15L onboard survey
respondents indicated that they were BIPOC and 60.6% low-income (respectively 7.6% and 8.6%
higher than the onboard survey average). These findings underscore the continued importance of
maintaining both onboard and off-board cash payment options until access to and adoption of other
payment methods become more widespread.

Actions
Based on this analysis, RTD staff intend to take the following actions to ensure its future TVM
modernization strategy centers equity and accessibility.
Reduction of TVMs at Existing Stations
Equity impacts from the proposed TVM reduction are predicted to be minimal. Concerns are also
assuaged by the fact that since 2024:
•
•
•
•
•
•

Approximately 90% of all transactions were by card, and RTD will implement a tap-to-pay
system in the fourth quarter of 2025
More than two-thirds of all transactions took place at two stations (Denver Airport Station and
Denver Union Station)
At several stations, the majority of fares are collected at a minority of TVMs (e.g., use among
TVMs at stations is not evenly distributed)
Nearly all stations have seen reduced TVM use since 2021
In 2024, total sales on TVMs proposed for removal at stations in BIPOC census block groups
are almost 50% less than those in non-BIPOC census block groups
In 2024, total sales on TVMs proposed for removal at stations in low-income census block
groups are only slightly higher (8.8%) than those in non-low-income census block groups

As a result, staff support systemwide reduction of TVMs, provided each station (excluding Flatiron Flyer
stations) retains at least one, and 13 higher-priority stations should retain two TVMs based on equity
and accessibility metrics. Specifically, staff propose that stations with numerous existing TVMs receiving
a composite index score of four and above retain two TVMs. This includes Lakewood●Wadsworth,
Decatur●Federal, Aurora Metro Center, 40th●Colorado and 61st●Peña Stations. In addition, the
accessibility review reiterates the need for two TVMs at Lakewood●Wadsworth Station and indicates
that RTD should retain additional TVMs at Colfax, Peoria, Auraria West, Clear Creek●Federal, I25●Broadway, Southmoor, Wheat Ridge●Ward Road and Louisiana●Pearl stations. The accessibility
review also specified that any TVM removed at Downtown Littleton Station should be the upper TVM,
10

The 15/15L onboard survey is used for comparison given its similarity to the upcoming Colfax BRT routing.

14

rtd-denver.com

Packet Pg. 28

Attachment: Attachment A - Ticket Vending Machines Modernization - Title VI Fare Equity Analysis (5226 : Ticket Vending Machines

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

since the lower TVM would better maintain station accessibility (see below for more information on
TVM replacement).
In sum, the total number of TVMs removed would drop from 74 to 61 (excluding the 15 Flatiron Flyer
stops).
Replacement of Machines with Small Footprint TVMs
Staff support replacement of remaining TVMs with small footprint TVMs if RTD procures models that
accept and dispense cash, given that BIPOC and low-income populations are more likely to be
unbanked and reliant on cash.11 Staff discourage the agency from adopting a change-free or changelimited TVM network.
RTD must also ensure that smaller footprint TVMs meet language access and accessibility needs by
providing digital translations in RTD’s top safe harbor languages, displaying instructions in English and
Spanish and offering Braille and audible guidance where applicable.
Removal of all TVMs at Flatiron Flyer Stations
Staff do not foresee equity impacts from removing the 15 TVMs at Flatiron Flyer stations, especially
given their redundancy with customer ability to pay via cash or card on board the Flatiron Flyers
themselves. Nevertheless, there may be other reasons to keep TVMs at Flatiron Flyer stations. For
example, TVMs at Downtown Boulder and US 36●Table Mesa stations netted over $93,000 and
$140,000 in 2024 sales, respectively. This level of sales could indicate a need to keep these machines.
Further, with East Colfax BRT and Federal BRT slated to receive TVMs, it may make sense to keep
TVMs along the Flatiron Flyer. In other words, the decision may hinge on revenue or other
considerations rather than equity. Staff leave this decision-making to other agency staff.
Optimize the Number of TVMs for East Colfax BRT
In compliance with the FTA Small Starts Grant funding for the East Colfax Bus Rapid Transit (BRT)
project, staff support the addition of a cash-capable TVM at each East Colfax BRT stop, bidirectionally.
This will, in turn, enable access to off-board fare purchases for all RTD customers and support one of
RTD’s most diverse and cash-reliant population segments.
Place New TVMs at Denver Union Station Bus Concourse and Civic Center Station
RTD intends to develop a closed-system design for the Denver Union Station Bus Concourse. Six TVMs
will be needed to support this design. In addition, RTD staff requested installing one TVM at Civic
Center Station. These directives will be incorporated into the agency’s overall TVM strategy following
the review and adoption of this analysis.

Fare Equity Analysis: TVM Modernization

1.D.2.a

Public Engagement
RTD will implement a coordinated outreach approach to inform customers about the reduction and
replacement of TVMs. The focus will be on transparency and accessibility, ensuring customers
understand what changes are coming and when they will occur. The Transit Equity Office and
Communications and Engagement Department will work together to ensure that customers, particularly
those who rely on cash or station-based fare payment, are aware of the planned changes and available
alternatives.
The outreach strategy will explore several approaches to reach customers effectively. Staff will look
into deploying contractors and staff to rail stations to provide direct information, answer questions and
distribute materials. The Marketing Department will explore producing decals and informational signage
at stations and onboard rail vehicles to notify customers of upcoming changes. The team will consider
developing printed materials such as rack cards or flyers summarizing the TVM reduction and
replacement process, including available payment options and project timelines. RTD will also explore
creating a dedicated webpage that outlines TVM updates, replacement schedules and fare payment
options such as MyRide and retail network cash-loading locations.
Additional communication tactics will include bilingual Read-n-Ride articles, social media updates and
news releases to ensure broad awareness. The Transit Equity Office will continue multilingual
engagement at community events to share information directly with customers, particularly BIPOC,
low-income and unbanked populations who may be more impacted by cash-related changes.
Through these combined efforts, RTD aims to provide clear, timely and equitable information about
the TVM reduction and replacement project across multiple communication channels. Because the work
is not slated to begin until 2026, the outreach approach may be modified as the project progresses.

Conclusion
RTD’s analysis concludes that the proposed TVM modernization plan maintains equitable fare access
across the system. Systemwide usage of TVMs remains low, with no evidence of DI on BIPOC customers
or DB on low-income customers. A station-level review identified 13 locations for retaining two
machines to ensure accessibility and service equity. The proposed removal of all Flatiron Flyer TVMs
presents minimal equity concern, as customers have onboard cash and MyRide payment options. For
the East Colfax BRT corridor, installing cash-capable TVMs at all stops will uphold Title VI and FTA
Small Starts requirements while supporting a diverse and transit-dependent customer base. Overall,
the proposed mitigations, including cash-capable machines and targeted station retention, align with
RTD’s fare modernization goals and Title VI equity standards.

Packet Pg. 37

Disparate Impact (DI)/Disproportionate Burden (DB) Policies

§ A fare change is considered to result in a:
• DI if BIPOC populations are affected 5% more than overall ridership
•If a DI is identified, RTD must:
§ Consider modifications to avoid, minimize or mitigate impacts
§ Prove substantial legitimate justification
§ Show no less-discriminatory alternatives
• DB if low-income populations are affected 5% more than the overall ridership
•If a DB is identified, RTD will:
§ Consider practicable changes to mitigate impact

Methodology – Composite Index Score
§ Station-Level Composite Index Score Data
• U.S. Census Bureau’s 2019-2023 American Community Survey (ACS)
• BIPOC and low-income populations
• Average ramp deployments per station/stop (January 2024 to January 2025)
• Average daily boardings (January 2024 to January 2025)
• TVM sales data (2024)
• RTD retail network (2024)
§ Each of the six metrics were weighted and scored using quartile rankings
§ Quartile scores combined into a composite score; higher scores indicate greater
equity impact and guide TVM retention or replacement

Added to Committee Agenda as a Director initiated request pursuant to RTD Board Bylaws Art. V,

MOTION
For the Board of Directors to adopt Resolution __ Series of 2025 which commits the Board and staff to
certain exploratory and planning efforts for fiscal years 2026 and 2027.
REPRESENTATIVES
Michael Guzman, Chair of the Finance and Planning Committee and Director (District C)
Karen Benker, Vice Chair of the Finance and Planning Committee and Director (District I)
Julien Bouquet, Board Chair and Director (District G)
PRESENTATION LENGTH
10 minutes
BACKGROUND
Each year the Board of Directors must adopt an annual budget and enact the associated resolution(s)
appropriating funds. The development and execution of the annual budget is guided by the Board’s
Fiscal Policy. The planning and preparation process for the annual budget is complex and extensive,
beginning many months before the Board is provided with a draft budget for review.
Because the Board is tasked with final approval of the budget, the budget is a reflection of the Board’s
priorities. However, under current practices which are driven by the complexities of the budget
development process and the Board’s Fiscal Policy, the Board does not review a draft budget until
October of each year. This timing creates procedural barriers in ensuring the annual budget
incorporates the priorities of the Board.
Given the challenges the Board faces ensuring its priorities are incorporated into the budget in the
limited time frame from when a draft budget is produced to when a final budget must be passed, the
Board must start its efforts early each year to ensure staff has appropriate direction from the Board in
the development of the proposed and final budgets.
DISCUSSION
The above Directors have identified certain planning efforts, commitments and areas of inquiry the
Board and staff should jointly pursue during fiscal years 2026 and 2027. These commitments will help

Packet Pg. 53

1.D.3

facilitate a budget development process which can more readily incorporate Board priorities. Directors
are asked to review, discuss, and vote on the attached resolution.
FINANCIAL IMPACT
The resolution calls for an increase in the available funds for the Partnership Program’s 2026 Call for
Projects for a maximum of $10 million in 2027 to fund existing and newly awarded projects. to be paid
out in 2027.

WHEREAS, the Board adopted the 2026 Fiscal Policy on October 28, 2025, and that Policy
contains Revenue, Expenditure, Grant, Capital Improvement, and Budget Policies; and
WHEREAS, the Revenue Policies indicate that the Board of Directors may consider potential fare

WHEREAS, the Expenditure Policies provide that RTD will implement the most cost-effective and
reliable methods of delivering public transit services; and
WHERAS, the agency’s highly popular Partnership Program was designed to be a cost-effective
means to expand reliable local transit options across the District; and
WHEREAS, the Grant Policies stipulate that the agency will draw apportioned grant funds as
quickly as possible for efficient working capital management; and
WHEREAS, RTD staff has submitted an application for $9.25 million in funding for 2026 and
requested consideration for an additional $40 million in funding for 2027 from the Clean Transit
Enterprise’s (CTE) Local Transit Operations Formula Program, and more funding opportunities
are anticipated; and
WHEREAS, the Colorado Transportation Investment Office (CTIO) will be administering
additional grant funding opportunities for transit agencies with funds generated by a Congestion
Impact Fee charged on rental cars; and
WHEREAS, the Capital Improvement Policies indicate capital asset investment decisions will be
prioritized in accordance with the Transit Asset Management (TAM) Plan and multi-year capital
project funding can be carried forward in accordance with Board policy; and
WHEREAS, carryforwards for existing capital projects and additional new capital projects
amount to $584 million in the agency’s proposed 2026 Budget; and
WHEREAS, the Budget Policies include a commitment to the preparation of a balanced budget
in which beginning reserves plus anticipated revenues is greater than or equal to expenditures,
and that the agency will monitor funding sources and expenditures and amend the budget, if
necessary; and

NOW THEREFORE BE IT RESOLVED by the Board of Directors of the Regional Transportation
District:
1. The Board will hold a Study Session in January of 2026 to assess potential fare changes
to optimize fare revenue, and any fare changes would be adopted in time for inclusion in
the proposed 2027 Budget.
2. Staff will increase available funds in the Partnership Program’s 2026 Call for Projects for
a maximum of $10 million in 2027 to fund existing and newly awarded projects.
3. The Board will amend the 2026 budget as soon as practicable following the award of any
funds from the Clean Transit Enterprise’s (CTE) Local Transit Operations Formula
Program.
4. Staff will develop a strategy to maximize potential awards from the CTE, which could
include up to 60% of the CTE’s anticipated $75 million 2026-2027 state fiscal year budget
for the Local Transit Operations Formula Funding Program.
5. Staff will develop a strategy to maximize potential grant awards from revenues generated
by the Congestion Impact Fee Program administered by the CTIO.
6. The Board directs staff to apply for maximal federal grant funding and expeditiously
provide notice to the Board of any additional federal grant funds awarded and accepted
by the agency.
7. The Board will hold at least one Study Session within the first quarter of 2026 to better
understand the agency’s current and projected capital and asset needs as well as to review
the status of the Facilities and Fleet Transition Plan.
8. The Board commits to identifying expense reductions and revenue increases sufficient to
produce a balanced budget in which the projected change in net position is greater than
or equal to zero in time for the development of the 2028 Proposed Budget.

BOARD OF DIRECTORS REPORT
Committee Meeting Date:
2026 Proposed Budget

Board Meeting Date:

RECOMMENDED ACTION
For the Board of Directors to adopt Resolution No. ___, Series of 2025 for the adoption of the 2026
Budget, Resolution No. ___, Series of 2025 for the 2026 Budget appropriation and Resolution No. ___,
Series of 2025 for the appropriation of 2026 previously appropriated capital.
STAFF REPRESENTATIVE
Kelly Mackey, Chief Financial Officer
Todd Nikkel, Senior Manager, Budget
PRESENTATION LENGTH
20 minutes
BACKGROUND
RTD’s 2026 Proposed Budget represents the agency’s financial plan for the coming year.
Pursuant to §29-1-105, Colorado Revised Statutes, RTD submitted the 2026 Proposed Budget to the
Board of Directors and made it available for public review by October 15, 2025, as required by §29-1106, Colorado Revised Statutes, providing a transparent appropriation process allowing for Board and
public input and comment. The 2026 Proposed Budget was made available in hardcopy form in the
Board Office and was posted on the RTD website with public notice posted in the Denver Post and
Boulder Daily Camera newspapers.
The 2026-2030 Five-Year Financial Forecast (FYFF), was presented to the Finance and Planning
Committee on September 9, 2025, as an action item and informed the development of the 2026
Proposed Budget.
This 2026 Proposed Budget action item includes a description of changes since the 2026 Proposed
Budget Finance and Planning Committee discussion item on October 14, 2025, and since the 2026
Proposed Budget study session on October 22, 2025.
DISCUSSION
The attached 2026 Proposed Budget document includes narratives describing the financial assumptions
and alignment with RTD’s Board-adopted Strategic Plan. In addition, tables, charts and commentary are
included for evaluation of the considerations included therein.

Packet Pg. 59

1.D.4

Staff have developed the 2026 Proposed Budget to conform with RTD’s Fiscal Policy directives while
best meeting the transit needs of its customers and communities at large.
FINANCIAL IMPACT
The 2026 Proposed Budget aligns 2026 expenditures with anticipated funding sources, while
maintaining 2026 Fiscal Policy requirements. RTD’s internal controls and budget appropriation process
provide those charged with leadership and governance responsibilities with the opportunity to adjust
and realign appropriations, within their delegated authority, throughout the fiscal year as necessary.

Packet Pg. 61

1.D.4.a

REGIONAL TRANSPORTATION DISTRICT

A RESOLUTION SUMMARIZING EXPENDITURES AND REVENUES AND ADOPTING A BUDGET
FOR THE REGIONAL TRANSPORTATION DISTRICT, FOR THE CALENDAR YEAR BEGINNING ON
THE FIRST DAY OF JANUARY 2026 AND ENDING ON THE LAST DAY OF DECEMBER 2026.
WHEREAS, The Board of Directors of the Regional Transportation District has appointed the
General Manager and CEO to prepare and submit a proposed budget to said governing body at
the proper time; and
WHEREAS, the General Manager and CEO has submitted a proposed budget to this governing
body on October 10, 2025 for its consideration; and
WHEREAS, upon due and proper notice, published and posted in accordance with the law, said
proposed budget was open for inspection by the public at a designated place, a public hearing
was held on December 2, 2025, and interested taxpayers were given the opportunity to file or
register any objections to said proposed budget; and
WHEREAS, whatever increases that have been made in expenditures, like increases were added
to revenues so that the budget remains balanced, as required by law.
NOW, THEREFORE, BE IT RESOLVED BY THE BOARD OF DIRECTORS OF THE REGIONAL
TRANSPORTATION DISTRICT:
Section 1. That the budget as submitted herein above is approved and adopted as the budget
of the Regional Transportation District for the year stated above.
Section 2. That the budget hereby approved and adopted shall be signed by the Chair and the
Secretary of the Board of Directors and made part of the public records of the District.

ADOPTED, this 2nd day of December 2025

Julien Bouquet, Chair

Attest

Chris Nicholson, Secretary

1.D.4.a

REGIONAL TRANSPORTATION DISTRICT

BE IT RESOLVED that the following sums of money or as much thereof as may be needed
or deemed necessary to defray all expenses and liabilities of the Regional Transportation District
be, and the same, are hereby appropriated for the corporate purposes and objectives of the
District hereinafter specified for the fiscal year commencing on the first day of January 2026,
and ending on the last day of December 2026.
BE IT RESOLVED that the appropriations made in this resolution shall become available
on the first day of January 2026, and if any multi-year appropriated capital project is not
completed within the calendar year, previously appropriated funds shall remain until completion
of the capital projects for which the funds are appropriated or for a period of three years,
whichever comes first, at which time unexpended locally funded balances shall revert to the
unrestricted fund balance. Federally funded capital projects in this context imply those discrete
multi-year activities or tasks which are components of those capital programs that comprise the
annual budget. Authority for the roll forward of the capital projects shall be subject to the
approval of the Board of Directors.

Passed and adopted by the Board of Directors of the Regional Transportation District on the 2nd
day of December 2025.

Julien Bouquet, Chair

Chris Nicholson, Secretary

2026 Proposed Budget Expenditure Categories
§ Salary and Benefits: Wages, overtime, benefits, salaried and union pension plan contributions,
defined contribution plan contributions, uniform allowance, tool allowance, compensated absences,
etc.

§ Purchased Transportation: Third-party transportation contracted services, including commuter rail
(excluding N Line), fixed route bus service, and complementary Paratransit service

§ Services: Supplemental premium service, software support, infrastructure as a service, software
subscription licenses, professional services, maintenance contracts, snow removal, security
contractors, etc.

§ Materials and Supplies: Revenue vehicle repair parts, low-value equipment, fuel, consumables, etc.
§ Other Expenses: Director travel/conferences/meals, employee travel, training, employee recognition,
contingency, etc.

Packet Pg. 83

Attachment: 2026 Proposed Budget [Revision 1] (5229 : 2026 Proposed Budget)

1.D.4.b

Reduced Capital • Room for More Operating Expense
§ Capital and Operating budgets serve different purposes
• Capital Expenditures (CapEx): One-time investments in long-lived assets (vehicles, facilities, etc.)
• Operating Expenditures (OpEx): Recurring costs to run the organization (wages, fuel, maintenance, etc.)
• These budgets are governed by different funding sources, accounting treatments, and sustainability
horizons

§ Lower CapEx does not increase spendable income
• Debt service and prior capital obligations remain fixed even if new projects are deferred
• Net revenue coverage ratios measure ability to meet debt service, not total spending
• Unbalanced budgets leave no capacity to reallocate spending toward recurring operations
November 11, 2025

19

Packet Pg. 84

Attachment: 2026 Proposed Budget [Revision 1] (5229 : 2026 Proposed Budget)

1.D.4.b

Reduced Capital • Room for More Operating Expense
Deferring capital projects does not equate to available funds for operations. Financial capacity is determined by
recurring revenue, debt service coverage, and ability to sustain operations.
Consideration

Capital Expenditure

Operating Expenditure

Nature

One-time investment

Recurring expense

Funding Source

Cash or debt

Operating revenue

Type

Asset (balance sheet)

Expense (income statement)

Impact

Depreciation / debt

Increases baseline cost structure

Budget Flexibility

State of good repair/safety or discretionary

Ongoing obligation

§ When Net Revenue Coverage falls below need, recurring cost increases worsen solvency
§ Reducing capital (asset) appropriation does not create new expense capacity
§ Restore balance through revenue improvement, efficiency, and coverage optimization
November 11, 2025

20

1.D.4.c

Fiscal Year 2026

January 1 – December 31, 2026
The Regional Transportation District (RTD) annually reviews, proposes,
and adopts a fiscal year budget and five-year financial forecast. This budget
book aligns RTD’s financial resources with the agency’s strategic plan and
primary objective of delivering transit services to the Denver metro area.

rtd-denver.com/budget

General Manager and CEO Message

A

4

1.D.4.c

General Manager and CEO Message

Debra A. Johnson

General Manager and CEO
• As RTD’s Transit Police Department continues to grow, the addition
of sworn officers better enables RTD-PD to build connections
and respond to calls throughout the agency’s service area. The
department has expanded its unhoused outreach coordinators and
added mental health clinicians, an approach pioneered in transit
policing by the Chief of Police and Emergency Management.
I extend my gratitude to each one of RTD’s employees, whose
commitment and dedication have made these gains possible. They
bring their best selves to work every day in public service and make
a meaningful difference in the health of the region. I acknowledge
the contributions of the agency’s Board of Directors and its labor
and contract partners. I also thank every customer, stakeholder, local
leader, and partner who engaged with RTD. The agency will continue
to be agile and flexible as it endeavors to meet the transit needs of
the individuals and communities within its service district.

FT

The Fiscal Year 2026 Budget encapsulates RTD’s financial plan for the
forthcoming year. This budget aligns with the agency’s 2021–2026
Strategic Plan, which outlines an ongoing commitment to the Strategic
Priorities of Community Value, Customer Excellence, Employee
Ownership, and Financial Success. With this commitment also comes
a continued emphasis on the strategic initiative of Customer and
Community Connections, alongside the newly introduced initiatives of
Enhancing Public Trust, Building a Better RTD, and Service Excellence.

A

This budget reflects an intentional focus on the four initiatives which
are interwoven into work plans across all agency departments and
divisions. RTD will continue to make meaningful progress toward
established success outcomes by centering its work on each of these
factors. While RTD’s Finance staff, Leadership Team, and subjectmatter experts provide overarching guidance, every employee carries
a responsibility and an opportunity to work in a manner that brings
about meaningful improvements.

D
R

RTD saw substantial milestones and successes in 2025 that were
the direct result of continued coordination by teams and individuals
throughout the agency. Both within and outside RTD, people took
note of the following achievements:

• Following the announcement in March 2022 of a robust plan and
timeline to “Reclaim Union Station,” a collaborative and multi-faceted
strategy aimed at enhancing safety and security at this multimodal
transit hub, RTD in April 2025 reported a nearly 60 percent
reduction in security-related calls and a three-year decrease in
reports of criminal activities at this location.
• Thanks to the diligence and care shown by nearly 100 RTD staff,
from rail laborers to track maintainers, in May the agency lifted the
last of 31 light rail speed restrictions that were put in place over the
prior year as RTD implemented more rigorous standards for regularly
inspecting its more than 120 miles of light rail track. During this
period, crews removed and replaced more than two miles of track
along the D, E, H, and R lines.
• RTD’s most recent annual customer and community surveys, the
results of which were announced in June, showed double-digit,
year-over-year improvements in perceptions of personal safety and
security, the cleanliness of buses and trains, on-time performance,
the frequency of service, and the agency’s overall value in the
Denver metro area.

More specifically, I express my sincere gratitude for CFO Kelly Mackey
who rolled up her sleeves upon her arrival to the organization in May
and has delved into the agency’s inner workings. Additionally, a big
thanks to Todd Nikkel, Senior Manager of Budget; Brenden Morgan,
Senior Manager of Debt and Investment; Heidi Wagner; Senior
Manager of Finance; and all of their respective teams for leaning in
and supporting this critical undertaking in these unpredictable times.
Lastly, my wholehearted appreciation to the Leadership Team who
served as thought partners while I grappled with difficult decisions
to ensure a balanced budget while retaining the agency’s power and
service levels.
RTD is comprised of people moving people and each of these
individuals makes lives better through connections. On behalf of this
entire team, I invite you to keep watching as we make our transit
system one that is better for all.
Sincerely,
Debra A. Johnson
General Manager and CEO

• In September, RTD began the next phase of its Downtown Rail
Reconstruction Project, which is rebuilding the agency’s oldest rail
infrastructure along its original light rail alignment. This work follows
a successful initial phase in 2024 that was completed weeks ahead
of schedule.

Over the last year, the Regional Transportation District continued
its efforts to maintain assets in a state of good repair, addressed
impediments to employee recruitment and retention efforts,
demonstrated its commitment to building customer and community
connections, and made notable gains in creating a welcoming
transit environment for employees and customers alike.

Agency Overview

RTD was created in 1969 by the Colorado
General Assembly to develop, operate, and
maintain a mass transportation system that
now benefits more than 3.1 million people in
the Denver metro area. With a service area
of 2,345 square miles, RTD provides bus, rail,
and paratransit services in all or part of eight
counties and more than 40 municipalities.
The agency’s services are delivered via 126
bus routes, six light rail lines, four commuter
rail lines, and paratransit mobility options.

RTD is headquartered in Denver and governed
by a publicly elected Board of Directors. Each of
the 15 Directors represent a geographic district
of approximately 200,000 constituents and
serve four-year terms.

Strategic Priorities
RTD’s four Strategic Priorities are the functional pillars used to plan, develop, evaluate, and measure
the agency’s overall performance throughout the life cycle of the 2021–2026 Strategic Plan.

RTD strives to be a strong
community partner, providing
value to customers as well as
to the broader Denver metro
region while sustaining
planet Earth.

Employee
Ownership

Customer
Excellence
RTD strives to consistently
deliver high-quality
customer service.

Financial Success

D
R

A

FT

RTD seeks to attract and
retain a highly skilled and
engaged workforce.

RTD takes very seriously
the management of all
financial resources.

1.D.4.c

Strategic Initiatives
Progress toward Strategic Priorities is measured by way of key operational performance data and responses
to annual employee, customer, and community surveys. This information is then used to identify areas of
opportunity for improvement. Strategic Initiatives and associated tactics are developed each year to improve
agency performance in those specific areas.

RTD seeks to bolster trust among
the communities served by
demonstrating good stewardship of
taxpayer dollars and transit assets.

Building a Better RTD

A

FT

RTD is future-focused on the
advancement of its people
power and the enhancement
of its infrastructure.

D
R

RTD recognizes the importance
of reliably delivering highquality transit service to
support the mobility needs
of transit customers.

Customer and
Community
Connections

RTD is committed to fostering
community building and enriching
the customer experience.

Fiscal Year 2026 Budget Book

Attachment: Budget_Book_2026_Corrected_2025-11-10 (5229 : 2026 Proposed Budget)

This Budget Book contains RTD’s Annual Budget for Fiscal Year 2026, which runs from January 1, 2026, through
December 31, 2026. The information contained herein provides RTD’s Board of Directors, customers, community
partners, elected officials, and other stakeholders with a comprehensive summary of the agency’s plans to use
its financial resources to improve regional mobility options and enhance services in 2026.

25117
Packet Pg.

1.D.4.c

Fiscal and Budgetary Policies
Annually, the Board of Directors consider the RTD Fiscal Policy for the following year. Development of the
agency’s Budget adheres to this Fiscal Policy. The 2026 Fiscal Policy Action Item is scheduled for the Board
meeting on October 28, 2025. The 2026 Budget Policy, which is derived from the 2025 Fiscal Policy, is
summarized as follows:

• The Board will review and adopt
the RTD Fiscal Policy

FT

• There shall be a budgetary
monitoring system that charges
expenditures against
approved appropriations

D
R

A

• The Budget shall be summarized
for adoption purposes
• Budget preparation shall follow
Generally Accepted Accounting
Principles (GAAP)
• A balanced budget is required in
which revenues plus reserves
are greater than or equal
to expenditures
• Procedures that postpone
funding of necessary
expenditures, such as preventive
maintenance or equipment
replacement, will be avoided
• Conservative revenue estimates
that are reasonable shall be used
• RTD will monitor the Budget and
adjust as necessary

Attachment: Budget_Book_2026_Corrected_2025-11-10 (5229 : 2026 Proposed Budget)

• RTD shall comply with Colorado
Local Government Budget Law

• RTD shall prepare a Five-Year
Financial Forecast
• Budgets shall support the goals
and objectives of RTD
